<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#B0C4DE;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;font-size:110%;">Manpower</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Military age</th>
<td>17 with parental consent, 18 for voluntary service. Maximum age for enlistment is 35 for the Army,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goarmy.com_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-goarmy.com-1">[1]</a></sup> 28 for the Marines, 34 for the Navy, 39 for the Air Force,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and 27 for the Coast Guard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCGcareers_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-USCGcareers-3">[3]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Active personnel</th>
<td>1,301,300<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_military_and_paramilitary_personnel" title="List of countries by number of military and paramilitary personnel">ranked 2nd</a>)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Reserve personnel</th>

<li><a href="/wiki/Operation_Enduring_Freedom_%E2%80%93_Trans_Sahara" title="Operation Enduring Freedom – Trans Sahara">Trans Sahara</a></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a><br/>

<th scope="row">Ranks</th>
<td>
<p><b>Army</b><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_officer_rank_insignia" title="United States Army officer rank insignia">Army officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Warrant_officer_(United_States)" title="Warrant officer (United States)">Army warrant officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_enlisted_rank_insignia" title="United States Army enlisted rank insignia">Army enlisted</a></span><br/>
<b>Marines</b><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_rank_insignia" title="United States Marine Corps rank insignia">Marine Corps officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Warrant_officer_(United_States)" title="Warrant officer (United States)">Marine Corps warrant officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_rank_insignia" title="United States Marine Corps rank insignia">Marine Corps enlisted</a></span><br/>
<b>Navy</b><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_officer_rank_insignia" title="United States Navy officer rank insignia">Navy officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Warrant_officer_(United_States)" title="Warrant officer (United States)">Navy warrant officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Navy_enlisted_rates" title="List of United States Navy enlisted rates">Navy enlisted</a></span><br/>
<b>Air Force</b><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_officer_rank_insignia" title="United States Air Force officer rank insignia">Air Force officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_enlisted_rank_insignia" title="United States Air Force enlisted rank insignia">Air Force enlisted</a></span><br/>
<b>Coast Guard</b><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard_officer_rank_insignia" title="United States Coast Guard officer rank insignia">Coast Guard officer</a></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Warrant_officer_(United_States)" title="Warrant officer (United States)">Coast Guard warrant officer</a></span><br/></p>
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Coast_Guard_enlisted_ranks" title="List of United States Coast Guard enlisted ranks">Coast Guard enlisted</a></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>United States Armed Forces</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> are the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal</a> <a href="/wiki/Armed_forces" title="Armed forces">armed forces</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. They consist of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">Marine Corps</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">Air Force</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">Coast Guard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> is the military's overall head, and helps form military policy with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">U.S. Department of Defense</a> (DoD), a <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_executive_departments" title="United States federal executive departments">federal executive department</a>, acting as the principal organ by which military policy is carried out.</p>
<p>From the time of its inception, the military played a decisive role in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States" title="History of the United States">history of the United States</a>. A sense of national unity and identity was forged as a result of victory in the <a href="/wiki/First_Barbary_War" title="First Barbary War">First Barbary War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Barbary_War" title="Second Barbary War">Second Barbary War</a>. Even so, the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founders</a> were suspicious of a permanent military force. It played an important role in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, where leading generals on both sides were picked from members of the United States military. Not until the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> did a large standing army become officially established. The <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Act_of_1947" title="National Security Act of 1947">National Security Act of 1947</a>, adopted following World War II and during the Cold War's onset, created the modern U.S. military framework; the Act merged previously Cabinet-level <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">Department of War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Navy" title="United States Department of the Navy">Department of the Navy</a> into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">National Military Establishment</a> (renamed the Department of Defense in 1949), headed by the Secretary of Defense; and created the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Air_Force" title="United States Department of the Air Force">Department of the Air Force</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council" title="United States National Security Council">National Security Council</a>.</p>
<p>The U.S. military is one of the largest militaries in terms of number of personnel. It draws its personnel from a large pool of paid <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_military" title="Volunteer military">volunteers</a>; although <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_States" title="Conscription in the United States">conscription</a> has been used in the past in various times of both war and peace, it has not been used since 1972. As of 2016, the United States spends about $580.3 billion annually to fund its military forces and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_on_Terrorism" title="War on Terrorism">Overseas Contingency Operations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup> Put together, the United States constitutes roughly <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures" title="List of countries by military expenditures">40 percent of the world's military expenditures</a>. For the period 2010–14, the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_International_Peace_Research_Institute" title="Stockholm International Peace Research Institute">Stockholm International Peace Research Institute</a> (SIPRI) found that the United States was the world's largest exporter of major arms, accounting for 31 per cent of global shares. The United States was also the world's eighth largest importer of major weapons for the same period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-8">[8]</a></sup> The U.S. military has significant capabilities in both defense and <a href="/wiki/Power_projection" title="Power projection">power projection</a> due to its large budget, resulting in advanced and powerful equipment, and its widespread deployment of force around the world, including about 800 military bases in foreign locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_States" title="Military history of the United States">Military history of the United States</a></div>
<p>The history of the U.S. military dates to 1775, even before the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> marked the establishment of the United States. The <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/Continental_Navy" title="Continental Navy">Continental Navy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Marines" title="Continental Marines">Continental Marines</a> were created in close succession by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> in order to defend the new nation against the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>.</p>
<p>These forces demobilized in 1784 after the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> ended the War for Independence. The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> created the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> on 3 June 1784, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> created the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> on 27 March 1794, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">United States Marine Corps</a> on 11 July 1798. All three services trace their origins to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> (on 14 June 1775), the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Navy" title="Continental Navy">Continental Navy</a> (on 13 October 1775) and the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Marines" title="Continental Marines">Continental Marines</a> (on 10 November 1775), respectively. The 1787 adoption of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a> gave the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> the power to "raise and support armies", "provide and maintain a navy", and to "make rules for the government and regulation of the land and naval forces", as well as the power to <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_war_by_the_United_States" title="Declaration of war by the United States">declare war</a>. The United States President is the U.S. military's <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a>.</p>
<p>Rising tensions at various times with <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> and France and the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> and <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> quickened the development of the U.S. Navy (established 13 October 1775) and the United States Marine Corps (established 10 November 1775). The U.S. Coast Guard dates its origin to the founding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revenue_Cutter_Service" title="Revenue Cutter Service">Revenue Cutter Service</a> on 4 August 1790; that service merged with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Life-Saving_Service" title="United States Life-Saving Service">United States Life-Saving Service</a> in 1915 to establish the Coast Guard. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> was established as an independent service on 18 September 1947; it traces its origin to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Aeronautical_Division,_U.S._Signal_Corps" title="Aeronautical Division, U.S. Signal Corps">Aeronautical Division, U.S. Signal Corps</a> in 1907 and was part of the Army before becoming an independent service.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reserve_components_of_the_United_States_armed_forces#Mobilization" title="Reserve components of the United States armed forces">reserve branches</a> formed a military <a href="/wiki/Strategic_reserve" title="Strategic reserve">strategic reserve</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, to be called into service in case of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine's <a href="/wiki/Mark_Thompson_(reporter)" title="Mark Thompson (reporter)">Mark Thompson</a> has suggested that with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_War_on_Terrorism" title="Global War on Terrorism">War on Terror</a>, the reserves deployed as a single force with the active branches and America no longer has a strategic reserve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Command_structure">Command structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Command structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_National_Command.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1767" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/US_National_Command.png/220px-US_National_Command.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/US_National_Command.png/330px-US_National_Command.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/US_National_Command.png/440px-US_National_Command.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_National_Command.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Structure of the National Command Authority</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organizational_structure_of_the_United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="Organizational structure of the United States Department of Defense">Organizational structure of the United States Department of Defense</a></div>
<p>Command over the armed forces is established in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>. The sole power of command is vested in the President by <a href="/wiki/Article_Two_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article Two of the United States Constitution">Article II</a> as Commander-in-Chief. The Constitution also allows for the creation of "executive Departments" headed "principal officers" whose opinion the President can require. This allowance in the Constitution formed the basis for creation of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> in 1947 by the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Act_of_1947" title="National Security Act of 1947">National Security Act</a>. The Defense Department is headed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">Secretary of Defense</a>, who is a civilian and member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Cabinet" title="United States Cabinet">Cabinet</a>. The Defense Secretary is second in the military's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain_of_command" title="Chain of command">chain of command</a>, just below the President, and serves as the principal assistant to the President in all defense-related matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Together, the President and the Secretary of Defense comprise the <a href="/wiki/National_Command_Authority" title="National Command Authority">National Command Authority</a>, which by law, is the ultimate lawful source of military orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>To coordinate military strategy with political affairs, the President has a <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council" title="United States National Security Council">National Security Council</a> headed by the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_(United_States)" title="National Security Advisor (United States)">National Security Advisor</a>. The collective body has only advisory power to the President, but several of the members who statutorily comprise the council (the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Energy" title="United States Secretary of Energy">Secretary of Energy</a>, and the Secretary of Defense) possess executive authority over their own departments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Just as the President and the Secretary of Defense are in charge of the entire military establishment, maintaining <a href="/wiki/Civilian_control_of_the_military" title="Civilian control of the military">civilian control of the military</a>, so too are each of the Defense Department's <a href="/wiki/Organizational_structure_of_the_United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="Organizational structure of the United States Department of Defense">constitutive military departments</a> headed by civilians. The five branches are organized into three departments, each with civilian heads. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Army" title="United States Department of the Army">Department of the Army</a> is headed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Army" title="United States Secretary of the Army">Secretary of the Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Navy" title="United States Department of the Navy">Department of the Navy</a> is headed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Air_Force" title="United States Department of the Air Force">Department of the Air Force</a> is headed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Air_Force" title="United States Secretary of the Air Force">Secretary of the Air Force</a>. The Marine Corps is organized under the Department of the Navy. The Coast Guard is not under the administration of the Defense Department, but the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Homeland_Security" title="United States Department of Homeland Security">Department of Homeland Security</a> and receives its operational orders from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Homeland_Security" title="United States Secretary of Homeland Security">Secretary of Homeland Security</a>. However, the Coast Guard may be transferred to the Department of the Navy by the President or Congress during a time of war, thereby placing it within the Defense Department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The President, Secretary of Defense, and other senior executive officials are advised by a seven-member <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>, which is headed by the <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>, the highest-ranking officer in the United States military, and the <a href="/wiki/Vice_Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Vice Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Vice Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JCS01_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-JCS01-20">[20]</a></sup> The rest of the body is composed of the heads of each of the Defense Department's service branches (the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Army">Chief of Staff of the Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Naval_Operations" title="Chief of Naval Operations">Chief of Naval Operations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commandant_of_the_Marine_Corps" title="Commandant of the Marine Corps">Commandant of the Marine Corps</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Air_Force" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Air Force">Chief of Staff of the Air Force</a>) as well as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_National_Guard_Bureau" title="Chief of the National Guard Bureau">Chief of the National Guard Bureau</a>. Although commanding one of the five military branches, the <a href="/wiki/Commandant_of_the_Coast_Guard" title="Commandant of the Coast Guard">Commandant of the Coast Guard</a> is not a member of the Joint Chiefs of Staff. Despite being composed of the highest-ranking officers in each of the respective branches, the Joint Chiefs of Staff does not possess operational command authority. Rather, the <a href="/wiki/Goldwater%E2%80%93Nichols_Act" title="Goldwater–Nichols Act">Goldwater-Nichols Act</a> charges them only with advisory power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CJCS_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-CJCS-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>All of the branches work together during operations and joint missions in <a href="/wiki/Unified_Combatant_Command" title="Unified Combatant Command">Unified Combatant Commands</a>, under the authority of the Secretary of Defense with the exception of the Coast Guard. Each of the Unified Combatant Commands is headed by a Combatant Commander, a senior <a href="/wiki/Officer_(armed_forces)" title="Officer (armed forces)">commissioned officer</a> who exercises supreme command authority per <a href="/wiki/Title_10_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 10 of the United States Code">10 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/10/164" rel="nofollow">§ 164</a> over all of the forces, regardless of branch, within his geographical or functional command. By statute, the chain of command flows from the President to the Secretary of Defense to each of the Combatant Commanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In practice, the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff often acts as an intermediary between the Secretary of Defense and the Combatant Commanders.</p>
<p>All five armed services are among the seven <a href="/wiki/Uniformed_services_of_the_United_States" title="Uniformed services of the United States">uniformed services of the United States</a>, the two others being the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Public_Health_Service_Commissioned_Corps" title="United States Public Health Service Commissioned Corps">U.S. Public Health Service Commissioned Corps</a> (under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" title="U.S. Department of Health and Human Services">Department of Health and Human Services</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration_Commissioned_Officer_Corps" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration Commissioned Officer Corps">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration Commissioned Officer Corps</a> (under the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Commerce" title="United States Department of Commerce">Department of Commerce</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Budget">Budget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Budget">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_budget_of_the_United_States" title="Military budget of the United States">Military budget of the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="314" data-file-width="427" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png/200px-US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png/300px-US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png/400px-US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_defense_spending_1910_to_2007.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. military spending from 1910 to 2007, adjusted for inflation to 2003 dollars. The large spike represents <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> spending.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="424" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png/200px-US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png/300px-US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png/400px-US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_defense_spending_by_GDP_percentage_1910_to_2007.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
American defense spending by GDP percentage 1910 to 2007.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The United States has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures" title="List of countries by military expenditures">world's largest</a> <a href="/wiki/Military_budget_of_the_United_States" title="Military budget of the United States">military budget</a>. In the fiscal year 2016, $580.3 billion in funding were enacted for the Department of Defense (DoD) and for "Overseas Contingency Operations" in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_on_Terrorism" title="War on Terrorism">War on Terrorism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup> Outside of direct DoD spending, the United States spends another $218 to $262 billion each year on other defense-related programs, such as Veterans Affairs, Homeland Security, nuclear weapons maintenance, and the State Department.</p>
<p>By service, $146.9 billion was allocated for the Army, $168.8 billion for the Navy and Marine Corps, $161.8 billion for the Air Force and $102.8 billion for defense-wide spending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup> By function, $138.6 billion was requested for personnel, $244.4 billion for operations and maintenance, $118.9 billion for procurement, $69.0 billion for research and development, $1.3 billion for revolving and management funds, $6.9 billion for military construction, and $1.3 billion for family housing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In FY 2009, major defense programs saw continued funding:</p>
<ul>
<li>$4.1 billion was requested for the next-generation fighter, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-22_Raptor" title="Lockheed Martin F-22 Raptor">F-22 Raptor</a>, which was to roll out an additional 20 planes in 2009</li>
<li>$6.7 billion was requested for the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-35_Lightning_II" title="Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II">F-35 Lightning II</a>, which is still under development, but 16 planes were slated to be built</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Future_Combat_System" title="Future Combat System">Future Combat System</a> program is expected to see $3.6 billion for its development.</li>
<li>A total of $12.3 billion was requested for missile defense, including <a href="/wiki/Medium_Extended_Air_Defense_System" title="Medium Extended Air Defense System">Patriot CAP</a>, <a href="/wiki/MIM-104_Patriot" title="MIM-104 Patriot">PAC-3</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space-Based_Infrared_System" title="Space-Based Infrared System">SBIRS-High</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Loren Thompson, a defense analyst with the <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Institute" title="Lexington Institute">Lexington Institute</a>, has blamed the "vast sums of money" squandered on cutting-edge technology projects that were then canceled on shortsighted political operatives who lack a long-term perspective in setting requirements. The result is that the number of items bought under a given program are cut. The total development costs of the program are divided over fewer platforms, making the per-unit cost seem higher and so the numbers are cut again and again in a death spiral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Although the United States was the world's biggest exporter of major weapons in 2010–14, the US was also the world's eight biggest importer during the same period. US arms imports increased by 21 per cent between 2005–2009 and 2010–14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cost containment measures in the <a href="/wiki/Patient_Protection_and_Affordable_Care_Act" title="Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act">Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_the_Obama_administration" title="Energy policy of the Obama administration">Obama administration's energy policy</a> will play critical determining roles because health care and fuel costs are the two fastest-growing segments of the defense budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="327" data-file-width="429" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png/200px-Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png/300px-Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png/400px-Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Active_duty_end_strength_graph.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Active duty U.S. military personnel from 1950 to 2003. The two peaks correspond to the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The projected active duty end strength in the armed forces for FY 2017 was 1,281,900 people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup> with an additional 801,200 people in the seven reserve components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup> It is an all-volunteer military, but conscription through the <a href="/wiki/Selective_Service_System" title="Selective Service System">Selective Service System</a> can be enacted at the President's request and Congress' approval. All males ages 18 through 25 who are living in the United States are required to register with the Selective Service for a potential future draft.</p>
<p>The U.S. military is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_active_troops" title="List of countries by number of active troops">the world's second largest</a>, after China's <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a>, and has troops deployed around the globe.</p>
<p>From 1776 until September 2012, a total of 40 million people have served in the United States Armed Forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FY 2017 DoD budget request<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-4">[4]</a></sup> plan calls for an active duty end strength of 1,281,900, a decrease of 19,400 from the 2016 baseline as a result of decrements in the Army (15,000 fewer personnel) and Navy (4,400 fewer personnel) strength. The budget request also calls for a reserve component end strength of 801,200, a decrease of 9,800 personnel.</p>
<p>As in most militaries, members of the U.S. military hold a rank, either that of <a href="/wiki/Officer_(armed_forces)" title="Officer (armed forces)">officer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warrant_Officer_(United_States)" title="Warrant Officer (United States)">warrant</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Enlisted_rank" title="Enlisted rank">enlisted</a>, to determine seniority and eligibility for promotion. Those who have served are known as <a href="/wiki/Veteran#United_States" title="Veteran">veterans</a>. Rank names may be different between services, but they are matched to each other by their corresponding <a href="/wiki/U.S._uniformed_services_pay_grades" title="U.S. uniformed services pay grades">paygrade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Officers who hold the same rank or paygrade are distinguished by their date of rank to determine seniority, while officers who serve in certain positions of office of importance set by law, outrank all other officers in active duty of the same rank and paygrade, regardless of their date of rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senior_officers_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-senior_officers-28">[28]</a></sup> Currently, only one in four persons in the United States of the proper age meet the moral, academic and physical standards for military service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel_in_each_service">Personnel in each service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Personnel in each service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
