<p>These numbers do not take into account the use of <a class="external text" href="http://www.acq.osd.mil/log/PS/psc.html" rel="nofollow">Private Military and Private Security Companies (PSCs)</a>. Quarterly PSC census reports are available for <a href="/wiki/United_States_Central_Command" title="United States Central Command">United States Central Command</a> (USCENTCOM)'s area of operations—i.e., Iraq and Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> As of March 2011, there were 18,971 private security contractor (PSC) personnel in Afghanistan working for DoD; in Iraq, there were 9,207 PSC personnel, down from a high of 15,279 in June 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> As of October 2012, in Afghanistan, there were 18,914 PSC personnel working for DoD; in Iraq, there were 2,116 PSC personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The total number of DoD contractors in Iraq and Afghanistan was more than 137,400; reported PSCs were only a part of the number.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel_stationing">Personnel stationing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Personnel stationing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_military_deployments" title="United States military deployments">United States military deployments</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overseas">Overseas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Overseas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:United_States_military_presence_in_other_countries" title="Category:United States military presence in other countries">United States military presence in other countries</a></div>
<p>As of 31 December 2010, U.S. armed forces were stationed in 150 countries; the number of non-contingent deployments per country ranges from 1 in <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a> to over 50,000 in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Some of the largest deployments are: 103,700 in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, 52,440 in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> <i>(see <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Army_installations_in_Germany" title="List of United States Army installations in Germany">list</a></i>), 35,688 in Japan (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Forces_Japan" title="United States Forces Japan">USFJ</a>), 28,500 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea" title="Republic of Korea">South Korea</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Forces_Korea" title="United States Forces Korea">USFK</a>), 9,660 in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and 9,015 in the United Kingdom. These numbers change frequently due to the regular recall and deployment of units.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Global_Military_Presence.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/US_Global_Military_Presence.svg/220px-US_Global_Military_Presence.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/US_Global_Military_Presence.svg/330px-US_Global_Military_Presence.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/US_Global_Military_Presence.svg/440px-US_Global_Military_Presence.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Global_Military_Presence.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US global military presence.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Altogether, 77,917 military personnel are located in Europe, 141 in the <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">former Soviet Union</a>, 47,236 in East Asia and the Pacific, 3,362 in North Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, and South Asia, 1,355 in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> and 1,941 in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a> excluding the United States itself.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Within_the_United_States">Within the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Within the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>Including U.S. territories and ships afloat within territorial waters</i></p>
<p>As of 31 December 2009, a total of 1,137,568 personnel were on active duty within the United States and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._territory" title="U.S. territory">territories</a> (including 84,461 afloat).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_Armed_Forces_Deployment_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-2009_Armed_Forces_Deployment-38">[38]</a></sup> The vast majority (941,629 personnel) were stationed at bases within the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">contiguous United States</a>. There were an additional 37,245 in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> and 20,450 in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>; 84,461 were at sea, 2,972 in <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, and 179 in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_personnel">Types of personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Types of personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enlisted">Enlisted</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Enlisted">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forcesus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Forcesus.jpg/220px-Forcesus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Forcesus.jpg/330px-Forcesus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Forcesus.jpg/440px-Forcesus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forcesus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>U. S. Armed Forces Career Center</i> in <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">Times Square</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USmilitary.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/USmilitary.JPG/220px-USmilitary.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/USmilitary.JPG/330px-USmilitary.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/USmilitary.JPG/440px-USmilitary.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USmilitary.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Service members of the U.S. at an <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a> event, L-R: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">U.S. Marine Corps</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a> personnel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prospective service members are often <a href="/wiki/Military_recruitment" title="Military recruitment">recruited</a> from high school or college, the target age ranges being 18–35 in the Army, 18–28 in the Marine Corps, 18–34 in the Navy, 18–39 in the Air Force, and 18–27 (up to age 32 if qualified for attending guaranteed "A" school) in the Coast Guard. With the permission of a parent or guardian, applicants can enlist at age 17 and participate in the <a href="/wiki/Delayed_Entry_Program" title="Delayed Entry Program">Delayed Entry Program</a> (DEP), in which the applicant is given the opportunity to participate in locally sponsored military activities, which can range from sports to competitions led by recruiters or other military liaisons (each recruiting station's DEP varies).</p>
<p>After enlistment, new recruits undergo <a href="/wiki/Recruit_training" title="Recruit training">basic training</a> (also known as "boot camp" in the Marine Corps, Navy and Coast Guard), followed by schooling in their primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_military_occupation_codes" title="United States military occupation codes">Military Occupational Specialty</a> (MOS) or <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Navy_ratings" title="List of United States Navy ratings">rating</a> at any of the numerous training facilities around the world. Each branch conducts basic training differently. Marines send all non-infantry MOS's to an infantry skills course known as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_School_of_Infantry" title="United States Marine Corps School of Infantry">Marine Combat Training</a> prior to their technical schools. Air Force Basic Military Training graduates attend Technical Training and are awarded an <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Specialty_Code" title="Air Force Specialty Code">Air Force Specialty Code</a> (AFSC) at the apprentice (3) skill level. All Army recruits undergo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_Combat_Training" title="Basic Combat Training">Basic Combat Training</a> (BCT), followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_Individual_Training" title="Advanced Individual Training">Advanced Individual Training</a> (AIT), with the exceptions of cavalry scouts, infantry, armor, combat engineers, and <a href="/wiki/Military_police#United_States" title="Military police">military police</a> recruits who go to One Station Unit Training (OSUT), which combines BCT and AIT. The Navy sends its recruits to Recruit Training and then to "A" schools to earn a rating. The Coast Guard's recruits attend basic training and follow with an "A" school to earn a <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Coast_Guard_ratings" title="List of United States Coast Guard ratings">rating</a>.</p>
<p>Initially, recruits without higher education or college degrees will hold the pay grade of E-1, and will be elevated to E-2 usually soon after basic training. Different services have different incentive programs for enlistees, such as higher initial ranks for college credit, being an <a href="/wiki/Eagle_Scout_(Boy_Scouts_of_America)" title="Eagle Scout (Boy Scouts of America)">Eagle Scout</a>, and referring friends who go on to enlist as well. Participation in DEP is one way recruits can achieve rank before their departure to basic training.</p>
<p>There are several different authorized pay grade advancement requirements in each junior-enlisted rank category (E-1 to E-3), which differ by service. Enlistees in the Army can attain the initial pay grade of E-4 (specialist) with a four-year degree, but the highest initial pay grade is usually E-3 (members of the Army Band program can expect to enter service at the grade of E-4). Promotion through the junior enlisted ranks occurs after serving for a specified number of years (which, however, can be waived by the soldier's chain of command), a specified level of technical proficiency, or maintenance of good conduct. Promotion can be denied with reason.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-commissioned_officers">Non-commissioned officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Non-commissioned officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>With very few exceptions, becoming a <a href="/wiki/Non-commissioned_officer" title="Non-commissioned officer">non-commissioned officer</a> (NCO) in the U.S. military is accomplished by progression through the lower enlisted ranks. However, unlike promotion through the lower enlisted tier, promotion to NCO is generally competitive. NCO ranks begin at E-4 or E-5, depending upon service, and are generally attained between three and six years of service. Junior NCOs function as first-line supervisors and squad leaders, training the junior enlisted in their duties and guiding their career advancement.</p>
<p>While considered part of the non-commissioned officer corps by law, senior non-commissioned officers (SNCOs) referred to as <a href="/wiki/Chief_petty_officer" title="Chief petty officer">chief petty officers</a> in the Navy and Coast Guard, or staff non-commissioned officers in the Marine Corps, perform duties more focused on leadership rather than technical expertise. Promotion to the SNCO ranks, E-7 through E-9 (E-6 through E-9 in the Marine Corps) is highly competitive. Personnel totals at the pay grades of E-8 and E-9 are limited by federal law to 2.5 percent and 1 percent of a service's enlisted force, respectively. SNCOs act as leaders of small units and as staff. Some SNCOs manage programs at headquarters level and a select few wield responsibility at the highest levels of the military structure. Most unit commanders have a SNCO as an enlisted advisor. All SNCOs are expected to mentor junior commissioned officers as well as the enlisted in their duty sections. The typical enlistee can expect to attain SNCO rank after 10 to 16 years of service.</p>
<p>Each of the five services employs a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senior_Enlisted_Advisor" title="Senior Enlisted Advisor">Senior Enlisted Advisor</a> at departmental level. This individual is the highest ranking enlisted member within that respective service and functions as the chief advisor to the service secretary, service chief of staff, and Congress on matters concerning the enlisted force. These individuals carry responsibilities and protocol requirements equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Three-star_rank" title="Three-star rank">three-star</a> <a href="/wiki/General_officer" title="General officer">general</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flag_officer" title="Flag officer">flag officers</a>. They are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Senior_Enlisted_Advisor_to_the_Chairman" title="Senior Enlisted Advisor to the Chairman">Senior Enlisted Advisor to the Chairman</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sergeant_Major_of_the_Army" title="Sergeant Major of the Army">Sergeant Major of the Army</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sergeant_Major_of_the_Marine_Corps" title="Sergeant Major of the Marine Corps">Sergeant Major of the Marine Corps</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Master_Chief_Petty_Officer_of_the_Navy" title="Master Chief Petty Officer of the Navy">Master Chief Petty Officer of the Navy</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chief_Master_Sergeant_of_the_Air_Force" title="Chief Master Sergeant of the Air Force">Chief Master Sergeant of the Air Force</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Master_Chief_Petty_Officer_of_the_Coast_Guard" title="Master Chief Petty Officer of the Coast Guard">Master Chief Petty Officer of the Coast Guard</a></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Warrant_officers">Warrant officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Warrant officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Additionally, all services except for the Air Force have an active <a href="/wiki/Warrant_officer_(United_States)" title="Warrant officer (United States)">warrant-officer</a> corps. Above the rank of Warrant Officer One, these officers may also be commissioned, but usually serve in a more technical and specialized role within units. More recently though they can also serve in more traditional leadership roles associated with the more recognizable officer corps. With one notable exception (Army helicopter and <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing</a> pilots), these officers ordinarily have already been in the military often serving in senior NCO positions in the field in which they later serve as a Warrant Officer as a technical expert. Most Army pilots have served some enlisted time. It is also possible to enlist, complete basic training, go directly to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warrant_Officer_Candidate_School_(U.S._Army)" title="Warrant Officer Candidate School (U.S. Army)">Warrant Officer Candidate school</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Rucker" title="Fort Rucker">Fort Rucker</a>, Alabama, and then on to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_school" title="Flight school">flight school</a>.</p>
<p>Warrant officers in the U.S. military garner the same customs and courtesies as commissioned officers. They may attend the officer's club, receive a command and are saluted by junior warrant officers and all enlisted service members.</p>
<p>The Air Force ceased to grant warrants in 1959 when the grades of E-8 and E-9 were created. Most non-flying duties performed by warrant officers in other services are instead performed by senior NCOs in the Air Force.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Commissioned_officers">Commissioned officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Commissioned officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Officers receive a <a href="/wiki/Officer_(armed_forces)" title="Officer (armed forces)">commission</a> in one of the branches of the U.S. military through one of the following routes.</p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Service_academies" title="United States Service academies">Service academies</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Point,_New_York" title="West Point, New York">West Point</a>, New York; <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annapolis" title="Annapolis">Annapolis</a>, Maryland; <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_Academy" title="United States Air Force Academy">United States Air Force Academy</a> at <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Springs,_Colorado" title="Colorado Springs, Colorado">Colorado Springs</a>, Colorado; the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard_Academy" title="United States Coast Guard Academy">United States Coast Guard Academy</a> at New London, Connecticut; and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Merchant_Marine_Academy" title="United States Merchant Marine Academy">United States Merchant Marine Academy</a> at <a href="/wiki/Kings_Point,_New_York" title="Kings Point, New York">Kings Point, New York</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reserve_Officers%27_Training_Corps" title="Reserve Officers' Training Corps">Reserve Officers' Training Corps</a> (ROTC)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Officer_Candidate_School" title="Officer Candidate School">Officer Candidate School</a> (OCS) (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Officer_Training_School" title="Officer Training School">Officer Training School</a> (OTS) in the Air Force): This can be through active-duty academies, or through state-run academies in the case of the Army National Guard.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Direct_commission_officer" title="Direct commission officer">Direct commission</a>: civilians who have special skills that are critical to sustaining military operations and supporting troops may receive direct commissions. These officers occupy leadership positions in law, medicine, dentistry, pharmacy, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Nurse_Corps" title="U.S. Army Nurse Corps">nurse corps</a>, intelligence, supply-logistics-transportation, engineering, public affairs, <a href="/wiki/Chaplain_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Chaplain Corps (United States Army)">chaplain corps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>, and others.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battlefield_commission" title="Battlefield commission">Battlefield commission</a>: Under certain conditions, enlisted personnel who have skills that separate them from their peers can become officers by direct commissioning of a commander so authorized to grant them. This type of commission is rarely granted and is reserved only for the most exceptional enlisted personnel; it is done on an <i>ad hoc</i> basis, typically only in wartime. No direct battlefield commissions have been awarded since the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. The Navy and Air Force do not employ this commissioning path.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limited_Duty_Officer" title="Limited Duty Officer">Limited Duty Officer</a>: Due to the highly technical nature of some officer <a href="/wiki/Billet" title="Billet">billet</a>s, the Marine Corps, Navy, and Coast Guard employ a system of promoting proven senior enlisted members to the ranks of commissioned officers. They fill a need that is similar to, but distinct from that filled by Warrant Officers (to the point where their accession is through the same school). While Warrant Officers remain technical experts, LDOs take on the role of a generalist, like that of officers commissioned through more traditional sources. LDOs are limited, not by their authority, but by the types of billets they are allowed to fill. However, in recent times, they have come to be used more and more like their more-traditional counterparts.</li>
</ul>
<p>Officers receive a commission assigning them to the officer corps from the president with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Foreign_Relations" title="United States Senate Committee on Foreign Relations">Senate</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Advice_and_consent" title="Advice and consent">consent</a>. To accept this commission, all officers must take an <a href="/wiki/United_States_Uniformed_Services_Oath_of_Office" title="United States Uniformed Services Oath of Office">oath of office</a>.</p>
<p>Through their careers, officers usually will receive further training at one or a number of the many <a href="/wiki/Staff_college" title="Staff college">staff colleges</a>.</p>
<p>Company grade officers in pay grades O-1 through O-3 (known as "junior" officers in the Navy and Coast Guard) function as leaders of smaller units or sections of a unit, typically with an experienced SNCO (or CPO in the Navy and Coast Guard) assistant and mentor.</p>
<p>Field grade officers in pay grades O-4 through O-6 (known as "senior" officers in the Navy and Coast Guard) lead significantly larger and more complex operations, with gradually more competitive promotion requirements.</p>
<p>General officers, or <a href="/wiki/Flag_officer" title="Flag officer">flag officers</a> in the Navy and Coast Guard, serve at the highest levels and oversee major portions of the military mission.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Five-star_ranking">Five-star ranking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Five-star ranking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Five-star_rank" title="Five-star rank">Five-star rank</a></div>
<p>These are ranks of the highest honor and responsibility in the armed forces, but they are almost never given during peacetime and only a very small number of officers during wartime have held a five-star rank:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fleet_admiral_(United_States)" title="Fleet admiral (United States)">Fleet Admiral</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Air_Force" title="General of the Air Force">General of the Air Force</a></li>
</ul>
<p>No corresponding rank exists for the Marine Corps or the Coast Guard. As with three- and four-star ranks, Congress is the approving authority for a five-star rank confirmation.</p>
<p>The rank of <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Armies" title="General of the Armies">General of the Armies</a> is considered senior to General of the Army, but was never held by active duty officers at the same time as persons who held the rank of General of the Army. It has been held by two people: <a href="/wiki/John_J._Pershing" title="John J. Pershing">John J. Pershing</a> who received the rank in 1919 after World War I, and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> who received it posthumously in 1976 as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Bicentennial" title="American Bicentennial">American Bicentennial</a> celebrations. Pershing, appointed to General of the Armies in active duty status for life, was still alive at the time of the first five-star appointments during World War II, and was thereby acknowledged as superior in grade by seniority to any World War II–era Generals of the Army. George Washington's appointment by <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Public_Law_94-479" title="s:Public Law 94-479">Public Law 94-479</a> to General of the Armies of the United States was established by law as having "rank and precedence over all other grades of the Army, past or present", making him not only superior to Pershing, but superior to any grade in the Army in perpetuity.</p>
<p>In the Navy, the rank of <a href="/wiki/Admiral_of_the_Navy_(United_States)" title="Admiral of the Navy (United States)">Admiral of the Navy</a> theoretically corresponds to that of General of the Armies, though it was never held by active-duty officers at the same time as persons who held the rank of Fleet Admiral. <a href="/wiki/George_Dewey" title="George Dewey">George Dewey</a> is the only person to have ever held this rank. After the establishment of the rank of Fleet Admiral in 1944, the Department of the Navy specified that the rank of Fleet Admiral was to be junior to the rank of Admiral of the Navy. However, since Dewey died in 1917 before the establishment of the rank of Fleet Admiral, the <a href="/wiki/Six-star_rank" title="Six-star rank">six-star rank</a> has not been totally confirmed.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_women">Role of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Role of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1400" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg/220px-C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg/330px-C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg/440px-C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C-130_-_First_all_female_crew.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From 2005, the first all female <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-130_Hercules" title="C-130 Hercules">C-130 Hercules</a> crew to serve a combat mission for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Army_Corps" title="Women's Army Corps">The Woman's Army Auxiliary Corps</a> was established in the United States in 1942. Women saw combat during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, first as nurses in the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> attacks on 7 December 1941. The <a href="/wiki/WAVES" title="WAVES">Woman's Naval Reserve</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Women%27s_Reserve" title="United States Marine Corps Women's Reserve">Marine Corps Women's Reserve</a> were also created during this conflict. In 1944 WACs arrived in the Pacific and landed in Normandy on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-Day" title="D-Day">D-Day</a>. During the war, 67 Army nurses and 16 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Nurse_Corps" title="United States Navy Nurse Corps">Navy nurses</a> were captured and spent three years as Japanese prisoners of war. There were 350,000 American women who served during World War Two and 16 were killed in action; in total, they gained over 1,500 medals, citations and commendations. <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Hall" title="Virginia Hall">Virginia Hall</a>, serving with the Office of Strategic Services, received the second-highest US combat award, the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Cross_(United_States)" title="Distinguished Service Cross (United States)">Distinguished Service Cross</a>, for action behind enemy lines in France.</p>
<p>After World War II, demobilization led to the vast majority of serving women being returned to civilian life. Law 625, <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Armed_Services_Integration_Act" title="Women's Armed Services Integration Act">The Women's Armed Services Act</a> of 1948, was signed by <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">President Truman</a>, allowing women to serve in the armed forces in fully integrated units during peace time, with only the WAC remaining a separate female unit. During the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> of 1950–1953 many women served in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_Army_Surgical_Hospital" title="Mobile Army Surgical Hospital">Mobile Army Surgical Hospitals</a>, with women serving in Korea numbering 120,000 during the conflict. During the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, 600 women served in the country as part of the Air Force, along with 500 members of the WAC, and over 6,000 medical personnel and support staff. The <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Ordnance Corps (United States Army)">Ordnance Corps</a> began accepting female missile technicians in 1974,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and female crewmembers and officers were accepted into <a href="/wiki/Field_Artillery_Branch_(United_States)" title="Field Artillery Branch (United States)">Field Artillery</a> missile units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="1903" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg/170px-Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg/255px-Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg/340px-Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leigh_Ann_Hester_medal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
SGT <a href="/wiki/Leigh_Ann_Hester" title="Leigh Ann Hester">Leigh Ann Hester</a>, awarded the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Star" title="Silver Star">Silver Star</a> for direct combat.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1974, the first six women aviators earned their wings as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a> pilots. The Congressionally mandated prohibition on women in combat places limitations on the pilots' advancement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> but at least two retired as captains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 1989, Capt Linda L. Bray, 29, became the first woman to command American soldiers in battle, during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_invasion_of_Panama" title="United States invasion of Panama">invasion of Panama</a>. The 1991 <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> proved to be the pivotal time for the role of women in the American Armed forces to come to the attention of the world media. There are many reports of women engaging enemy forces during the conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 2000s, women can serve on American combat ships, including in command roles. They are permitted to serve on submarines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> They are not permitted to participate in special forces programs such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navy_SEALs" title="Navy SEALs">Navy SEALs</a>. Women enlisted soldiers are barred from serving in Infantry, Special Forces, however female enlisted members and officers can hold staff positions in every branch of the Army except infantry and armor. Women can however serve on the staffs of infantry and armor units at Division level and above, and be members of Special Operations Forces. Women can fly military aircraft and make up 2% of all pilots in the U.S. Military. In 2003, Major <a href="/wiki/Kim_Campbell_(pilot)" title="Kim Campbell (pilot)">Kim Campbell</a> was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Flying_Cross_(United_States)" title="Distinguished Flying Cross (United States)">Distinguished Flying Cross</a> for landing her combat damaged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A-10_Thunderbolt_II" title="A-10 Thunderbolt II">A-10 Thunderbolt II</a> with no hydraulic control and only one functional engine after being struck by hostile fire over <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.</p>
<p>On 3 December 2015, United States of America Defense Secretary, Ashton Carter, announced that all military combat jobs would become available to women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> This gave women access to the roughly 10% of military jobs which were previously closed off due to their combat nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The decision gave military services until January 2016 to seek exceptions to the rule if they believe that certain jobs, such as machine gunner's, should be restricted to men only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49">[49]</a></sup> These restrictions were due in part to prior studies which stated that mixed gender units are less capable in combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[50]</a></sup> Physical requirements for all jobs remained unchanged, though.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[50]</a></sup> Many women believe this will allow for them to improve their positions in the military since most high-ranking officers start in combat positions. Since women are now available to work in any position in the military, female entry into the draft has been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sergeant <a href="/wiki/Leigh_Ann_Hester" title="Leigh Ann Hester">Leigh Ann Hester</a> became the first woman to receive the Silver Star, the third-highest US decoration for valor, for direct participation in combat. In Afghanistan, Monica Lin Brown was presented the Silver Star for shielding wounded soldiers with her body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In March 2012, the U.S. military had two women, <a href="/wiki/Ann_E._Dunwoody" title="Ann E. Dunwoody">Ann E. Dunwoody</a> and <a href="/wiki/Janet_C._Wolfenbarger" title="Janet C. Wolfenbarger">Janet C. Wolfenbarger</a>, with the rank of four-star general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In 2016, General <a href="/wiki/Lori_Robinson" title="Lori Robinson">Lori Robinson</a> became the first female officer to command a major <a href="/wiki/Unified_Combatant_Command" title="Unified Combatant Command">Unified Combatant Command</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USNORTHCOM" title="USNORTHCOM">USNORTHCOM</a>) in the history of the United States Armed Forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Order_of_precedence">Order of precedence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Armed_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Order of precedence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Under current Department of Defense regulation, the various components of the Armed Forces have a set order of seniority. Examples of the use of this system include the display of service flags, placement of Soldiers, Marines, Sailors, Airmen, and Coast Guardsmen in formation, etc. When the Coast Guard shall operate as part of the Navy, the cadets, United States Coast Guard Academy, the United States Coast Guard, and the Coast Guard Reserve shall take precedence, respectively, after the midshipmen, United States Naval Academy; the United States Navy; and Navy Reserve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Cadets, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Military_Academy" title="US Military Academy">U.S. Military Academy</a></li>
<li>Midshipmen, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Naval_Academy" title="US Naval Academy">U.S. Naval Academy</a></li>
<li>Cadets, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard_Academy" title="United States Coast Guard Academy">U.S. Coast Guard Academy</a> (when part of the Navy)</li>
<li>Cadets, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Air_Force_Academy" title="US Air Force Academy">U.S. Air Force Academy</a></li>
<li>Cadets, U.S. Coast Guard Academy (when part of the Department of Homeland Security)</li>
<li>Midshipmen, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Merchant_Marine_Academy" title="US Merchant Marine Academy">U.S. Merchant Marine Academy</a></li>
<li>United States Army</li>
<li>United States Marine Corps</li>
<li>United States Navy</li>
<li>United States Coast Guard (when part of the Navy)</li>
<li>United States Air Force</li>
<li>United States Coast Guard (when part of Homeland Security)</li>
<li>Army National Guard of the United States</li>
<li>United States Army Reserve</li>
<li>United States Marine Corps Reserve</li>
<li>United States Navy Reserve</li>
<li>United States Coast Guard Reserve (when part of the Navy)</li>
<li>Air National Guard of the United States</li>
<li>United States Air Force Reserve</li>
<li>United States Coast Guard Reserve (when part of Homeland Security)</li>
<li>Other training and auxiliary organizations of the Army, Marine Corps, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Merchant_Marine" title="United States Merchant Marine">Merchant Marine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_Air_Patrol" title="Civil Air Patrol">Civil Air Patrol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard_Auxiliary" title="United States Coast Guard Auxiliary">Coast Guard Auxiliary</a>, as in the preceding order. However, the Civil Air Patrol actually predates the Air Force as an independent service. The CAP was constituted through the Administrative Order 9 of 1 December 1941 and operated under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Air_Forces" title="U.S. Army Air Forces">U.S. Army Air Forces</a> during World War II. The CAP became the official civilian auxiliary of the newly independent USAF with the enactment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_Law_80-557" title="Public Law 80-557">Public Law 80-557</a> on 26 May 1948.</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Note:</b> While the U.S. Navy is "older" than the Marine Corps,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-precedence_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-precedence-57">[57]</a></sup> the Marine Corps takes precedence due to previous inconsistencies in the Navy's birth date. The Marine Corps has recognized its observed birth date on a more consistent basis. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> is considered to have established the Navy on 13 October 1775 by authorizing the purchase of ships, but did not actually pass the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Regulations#History" title="United States Navy Regulations">"Rules for the Regulation of the Navy of the United Colonies"</a> until 27 November 1775.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The Marine Corps was established by act of said Congress on 10 November 1775. The Navy did not officially recognize 13 October 1775 as its birth date until 1972, when then–<a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Naval_Operations" title="Chief of Naval Operations">Chief of Naval Operations</a> Admiral <a href="/wiki/Elmo_Zumwalt" title="Elmo Zumwalt">Elmo Zumwalt</a> authorized it to be observed as such.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-precedence_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-precedence-57">[57]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The United States Coast Guard has both military and law enforcement functions. <a href="/wiki/Title_14_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 14 of the United States Code">Title 14 of the United States Code</a> provides that "The Coast Guard as established 28 January 1915, shall be a military service and a branch of the armed forces of the United States at all times." Coast Guard units, or ships of its predecessor service, the Revenue Cutter Service, have seen combat in every war and armed conflict of the United States since 1790, including the Iraq War.</span></li>
